federal review of state decisions END
with few exceptions , congress has not given federal courts exclusive authority to enforce rights arising under federal law . END
to put it differently , state and federal courts have concurrent jurisdiction with respect to most claims of federal right . END
to insure uniformity in the meaning of national law , however , state interpretations are subject to supreme court review . END
it may be noted , UNK , that to UNK `` desegregation '' an UNK and former southern governor has urged congress to abolish this reviewing authority . END
the result , of course , would be that federal law inevitably would mean different things in different states . END
it would also probably mean different things within the same state -- depending upon what court ( state or federal ) rendered decision . END
we consider here only a few of many problems involved in this crucial UNK relationship . END
the first is that enforcement of national law in state litigation raises in reverse the old diversity puzzle of the relation of procedure to substance . END
subject to certain constitutional restraints in favor of fair trials , each level of government is free to devise its own judicial procedures . END
UNK who choose to assert federal claims in a state court go into that court subject to its rules of procedure . END
a similar canon applies to those who press state claims in federal tribunals , e.g. , in diversity cases . END
in an UNK controversy the state court followed established state procedure by UNK a vague complaint `` most strongly against '' the UNK . END
in other words the burden of pleading clearly rested upon the UNK by state law . END
the result was that the UNK case was dismissed . END
mr. justice black led a reversing majority : `` strict local rules of pleading cannot be used to impose unnecessary burdens upon rights of recovery authorized by federal law '' . END
here , as in the byrd case , another element of state procedure was UNK to federal UNK law . END
UNK frankfurter and jackson UNK : `` one state may cherish UNK more than another , one state may be more responsive than another to procedural reforms . END
if a UNK chooses to enforce a federal right in a state court , he cannot be heard to object if he is treated exactly as are UNK who press like claims arising under state law with regard to the form in which the claim must be stated -- the UNK , for instance , with which a cause of action must be described . END
federal law , though invoked in a state court , UNK the federal claim -- defines what gives a right to recovery and what goes to prove it . END
but the form in which the claim must be stated need not be different from what the state UNK in the enforcement of like obligations created by it , so long as a requirement does not add to , or UNK , the right as defined by federal law , nor burden the realization of this right in the UNK of litigation '' . END
another problem in the area of UNK relationships is this : what constitutes reversible error in a state decision ? ? END
UNK v. chicago involved a conviction for UNK conduct under a local ordinance . END
the conduct in question was a speech . END
the accused did not object to the trial court's charge to the jury that discourse `` may constitute a breach of the peace if it UNK the public to anger , invites dispute , brings about a condition of unrest . END

though the accused raised many other objections , he did not object on this crucial point at any stage of the proceedings . END
that is , he did not claim in any of the four courts through which his case progressed that the jury charge had denied him any federal right . END
how else can one explain , for example , allowing the survival of the right to UNK bond discount and premium ( section UNK ) , but not the right to UNK bond issue expenses ; ; END

there does not seem to be any reasonable basis for distinction either in terms of the nature of the tax attribute or in terms of UNK possibilities . END
with respect to items such as these the provisions of section UNK , viewed in historical perspective , suggest a rule requiring survival , whether the items are beneficial or detrimental to the surviving corporation . END
to this extent some stretching of the literal meaning of the committee report seems justified , since the literal meaning conflicts with the clear implication , if not the language , of the statute . END
it is not contended that section 381 should prescribe the survival of all of the UNK tax attributes . END
such an interpretation could not be justified by a construction of the statute alone ; ; END
it would certainly violate the intention of congress as expressed in the committee report ; ; END
and in at least one instance , involving refund claims , it might be contrary to another provision of the united states code . END
section 203 of the united states code UNK an assignment of a claim against the government unless made after it has been allowed , the amount due has been ascertained , and a warrant for its payment has been issued . END
if it were not for judicial development of certain exceptions , this section would UNK a suit for refund by an acquiring corporation for taxes paid by a transferor corporation , even though the reorganization meets the requirements of section 381(a) . END
a clearly recognized exception is a statutory merger or consolidation . END
the leading case , seaboard air line railway v. united states , held that the UNK could sue for a refund of taxes paid by the transferor , and it has been consistently followed . END
the court said the purpose of the section was principally to spare the government the embarrassment and trouble of dealing with several parties , one of them a stranger to the claim , and to prevent traffic in claims , particularly tenuous claims , against the government . END
neither reason , said the court , applied to the case at hand ; ; END
furthermore , congress could not be presumed to have intended to obstruct mergers approved by the states . END
other exceptions are assignments for the benefit of UNK , corporate UNK , transfers by descent , or transfers by UNK . END
exceptions are often classified as transfers by `` operation of law '' . END
a tax-free reorganization not UNK with the merger or consolidation statutes of the states involved is difficult to fit into an `` operation of law '' mold . END
although it is in some ways comparable to a voluntary sale of assets for cash , to which section 203 quite clearly applies , the courts and treasury have held that acquiring corporations in several types of UNK UNK may sue for refund of taxes paid by UNK . END
a recent case in point is mitchell UNK v. united states , in which a claim against the government was transferred first from a corporation to a partnership , whose partners were former stockholders , and then to another corporation formed by the partners . END
holding the final corporation entitled to sue on the claim , the court cited the seaboard , UNK trading , and UNK cases for the proposition that `` transfers by operation of law or in conjunction with changes of corporate structure are not assignments prohibited by the statute '' . END
in an earlier case , UNK & co. v. united states , an american corporation was formed for the purpose of acquiring the stock of a british corporation in exchange for its own stock and then UNK the british corporation . END
the UNK statute was held not to prevent the american corporation from UNK for a refund of taxes paid by the british corporation . END

the court , nevertheless , relied on the seaboard case and also mentioned that the UNK of the two corporations were the same . END
in substance , said the court , there was no transfer of equitable title . END
the treasury arrives at substantially the same conclusion , but skirts the problem of section 203 of the united states code . END
revenue ruling UNK provides that if the corporation against which a tax was assessed has since been liquidated by merger with a successor corporation , a claim for refund should be filed by the successor in the name and on behalf of the corporation which paid the tax , followed by the name of the successor corporation . END
proper evidence of the liquidation and succession must also be filed . END
if the succession is a matter of public record , UNK of the secretaries of state or other public officials having UNK of the documents will suffice ; ; END
if the succession is not of record , all documents relating to such succession , properly certified , are required . END
the former proof seems applicable to a statutory merger or consolidation , the latter to a contractual acquisition . END
the ruling would not , however , apply to an acquisition of assets for cash . END
a recent ruling , although rather UNK , cites and follows rev. UNK UNK . END
the ruling suggests also that it applies to either a statutory or contractual reorganization . END
hence , a successor corporation in a c reorganization appears entitled to sue for a refund of taxes paid by the merged corporation despite section 203 . END
in a b reorganization , followed by a section UNK liquidation , those cases which hold that section 203 is UNK to transfers in liquidation appear to permit the successor corporation to sue for refund of taxes paid by the transferor . END
in fact , a cash purchase of a corporation's stock followed by liquidation might also be an effective way to transfer a claim for refund if the UNK doctrine is not applied to eliminate the intermediate step . END
these results appear sound . END
as stated in seaboard and numerous other cases , the two primary reasons for the enactment of section 203 of the united states code were to prevent the government from having to deal with more than one claimant and to prevent the assignment of UNK claims on a UNK basis . END
the cases have allowed transfer of claims if beneficial ownership is not changed . END
the first reason would never apply to a reorganization transfer which meets the conditions of section 381(a) , which is the only type presently under discussion . END
section 381(a) applies only to a transfer by liquidation of a subsidiary owned to the extent of at least 80 per cent , a statutory merger or consolidation , an acquisition of substantially all a corporation's assets solely in exchange for voting stock , or a change of identity , form , or place of organization . END
in virtually every case the transferor corporation is liquidated , and its former stockholders either own outright , or have a continuing stock interest in , the assets which gave rise to the tax . END
in these circumstances the possibility of multiple or conflicting claims is exceedingly remote . END
furthermore , in a c reorganization the continuing interest of stockholders of the corporation which paid the tax must be greater than is necessary in a statutory merger , to which the statute is clearly UNK . END
nor is it at all likely that a `` desperate '' claim against the government will be assigned on a UNK basis in the guise of a tax-free reorganization . END
if the transferor has substantial assets other than the claim , it seems reasonable to assume no corporation would be willing to acquire all of its properties in the dim hope of collecting a claim for refund of taxes . END
if such an unlikely transaction were to take place , it would more logically be accomplished by a stock purchase , followed by the prosecution of the claim by the UNK subsidiary , followed by liquidation . END
in the rare case where a corporation's only substantial asset , or its most important one , is a claim for refund , perhaps its transfer should not be permitted , whether the reorganization takes the form of a statutory merger or of the acquisition of assets for stock . END
it appears , then , that although the matter is not dealt with in section UNK , a successor corporation in a reorganization of a type specified in section 381(a) is entitled to sue for refund of taxes paid by a transferor corporation . END
section 203 of the united states code has been interpreted as not applying to claims against the government transferred in tax-free UNK . END
the successor corporations have been held entitled to sue on such claims . END
other tax attributes of the transferor END
there are certain tax attributes of a corporation whose nature and effect might depend on the facts of the particular reorganization involved . END
for example , property `` used in the trade or business '' of a transferor corporation , as defined in section UNK , presumably would not retain its special status following a UNK reorganization if it is not so used in the business of the acquiring corporation . END
the parent of a group filing consolidated returns might be treated as the same corporation following a reorganization defined in section UNK , but as a different corporation for this purpose after a tax-free acquisition by another corporation which had not , for example , elected to file consolidated returns with its own UNK . END
similar considerations presumably made it difficult to prescribe a general rule where the acquired and acquiring corporations have different methods of accounting ( section UNK ) or depreciation ( section UNK ) . END
other sections of the 1954 internal revenue code provide for survival of certain of a UNK tax attributes following a tax-free reorganization . END
section UNK requires carryover of the transferor corporation's basis for property transferred , and section UNK provides for UNK on the UNK holding period for such property to that of the UNK . END
section UNK permits a person acquiring UNK facilities to elect to continue UNK over a UNK period . END
however , a similar privilege was not specifically provided in section UNK for a person acquiring emergency facilities . END
attributes similar to a loss carryover . END
there may be certain items which are quite similar to a net operating loss carryover or operating deficit and whose right to survive a reorganization should perhaps be subject to the conditions applicable to those items . END
for example , suppose another excess profits tax similar to prior laws is enacted , providing for carryover of excess profits credits . END
this carryover right has a number of things in common with a net operating loss carryover . END
it is an averaging device intended to ease the tax burden of UNK income ; ; END
it is a tax benefit which might be of substantial value to a corporation which expects to have a high excess profits tax . END
under the 1939 code this item was permitted to survive a tax-free reorganization in the stanton UNK case , but only over the dissent of judge learned hand , who wrote the majority opinion in the UNK case , a leading case requiring carryover of earnings and profits in a UNK reorganization . END
since this type of item was not in the statute when section 381 was enacted in 1954 , one cannot say with certainty what effect the enactment of that section should have . END
with respect to this type of item , one might properly apply the language of the committee report , quoted above , which UNK against using section 381 as a basis for treating other tax attributes not mentioned therein . END
actually , there do not presently appear to be items in the statute comparable to a net operating loss carryover . END
probably the primary reason for special treatment of a net operating loss carryover is the unique opportunity it presents for tax avoidance . END
reasons for selecting mail questionnaire method END
there were two methods that could have been used for conducting the study within the resources available : ( 1 ) interviews in depth with a few selected companies , and ( 2 ) the more limited UNK of a large number of companies by means of a mail questionnaire . END
while the method of interviewing a small number of companies was appealing because of the opportunity it might have furnished to probe fully the reasons and circumstances of a company's practices and opinions , it also involved the risk of paying undue attention to the unique and peculiar problems of just a few individual companies . END
as a result , it was decided that a mail questionnaire sent to a large number of companies would be more effective in determining the general practices and opinions of small firms and in UNK some of the fundamental and recurring problems of defense procurement that concern both industry and government . END
it was also hoped that responses to a mail questionnaire would suggest fruitful inquiries that might be made in subsequent studies of a more detailed nature . END
it is recognized that a mail questionnaire has inherent limitations . END
there is the danger that the questions will mean different things to different respondents . END
simple `` yes '' or `` no '' answers do not reveal the different shades of opinion that the various respondents may have . END
a respondent may want to make alternative answers because he does not know the precise circumstances assumed in the question . END
there is also the problem of the UNK frame of reference . END
is the respondent making a recommendation for his own benefit , for the benefit of his industry , for the benefit of a specific government department or service , for the benefit of the defense program , for the benefit of small business , or for the benefit of the taxpayers ? ? END
there is also the question of whether the respondent based his answers on factual information and carefully considered judgment , or whether his answers were casual UNK . END
finally , there is the question of how strongly an expressed opinion is held -- whether it is a firm opinion or one that the respondent favors only slightly over the alternatives . END
the research team was very mindful of these dangers and limitations of a mail questionnaire . END
under the circumstances , however , the team considered it would provide the most useful information at this point . END
in the preparation of the questionnaire the problems noted above were carefully considered , and the structure and UNK used were designed to minimize the effects of these limitations . END
the questionnaire was designed to UNK three types of information : ( 1 ) the facts regarding certain characteristics of the respondents , including their experience with , and interest in , securing defense business ; ; END
( 2 ) the actual selling and buying practices of the respondents ; ; END
and ( 3 ) the attitudes and opinions of the respondents concerning bidding procedures and the methods of UNK defense contracts . END
it was hoped that the facts concerning the characteristics and practices of the respondents would offer clues to the reasons why they took the positions and made the recommendations which they did . END
the major sections of the questionnaire ( see appendix b ) are devoted to the following : 1 . END
information for UNK respondents ( part a of the questionnaire ) 2 . END
characteristics of defense sales activities ( part b of the questionnaire ) 3 . END
respondents' practices in participating in advertised bidding for defense business ( part c of the questionnaire ) 4 . END
respondents' practices in participating in negotiated bidding for defense purposes ( part d of the questionnaire ) 5 . END
respondents' opinions regarding advertised bidding ( part e of the questionnaire ) 6 . END
respondents' opinions regarding negotiated bidding ( part f of the questionnaire ) 7 . END
respondents' preferences regarding the methods of UNK defense contracts ( part g of the questionnaire ) END
the questionnaire provided a place for the name of the respondent but stated that identification of the respondent was optional . END
the questionnaire also stated that , in any event , all replies would be treated confidentially . END
it is interesting to note that 75 per cent of those who returned the questionnaire identified themselves . END
preparation and UNK of the questionnaire END
the research team prepared and then revised the questionnaire over a period of six months . END
in june , 1960 , an early draft of the questionnaire , along with a cover letter , was mailed to fourteen companies in the state of washington . END
several days after the companies had received the questionnaire , members of the research team contacted the presidents of eleven of these companies in person or by phone to discuss any ambiguities or difficulties the UNK might have experienced in completing the questionnaire . END
this test resulted in further revisions of the questionnaire . END
the research team was concerned that responses from firms in the state of washington might not be typical of those throughout the country , or that the results might be different when no phone or personal follow-up was made . END
accordingly , another test of the UNK was made . END
the revised draft was mailed in july , 1960 , to 100 firms throughout the united states . END
fifty of the 100 firms were selected on a random basis from UNK names submitted by member companies of the aerospace industries association ( aia list ) and fifty were selected in a similar manner from a list of UNK names compiled by the research team from the thomas register ( tr list ) . END
the method of compiling the aia and tr lists will be described later . END
ten days after the questionnaires were mailed , follow-up airmail UNK were sent urging those companies which had not yet returned their questionnaires to do so at once . END
twenty-eight returns in all were received . END
the responses were carefully checked for obvious errors in the answers or for questions that were apparently not understood by the respondent . END
the cover letter , questionnaire , and follow-up postcard were then revised into final form ( see UNK a , b , and c ) . END
the objective of the study was to determine the opinions and practices of small firms selling to defense programs . END
the firms to receive the questionnaires were selected with this objective in mind . END
three lists of companies were made and used in the study . END
the first was a list of fourteen manufacturing companies located in the state of washington which were personally known to the research team to be active in defense work . END
the primary consideration in the compilation of this list was convenience in discussing the questionnaire with company officers . END
the second list was derived from a group of approximately 8,000 names supplied to the research team by the aerospace industries association . END
these names were secured from member companies by the association from the forty-four sources listed in appendix aj . END
each source selected from its approved UNK list about 200 firms which it believed to be small businesses that participated in the production of weapons and weapon support systems . END
where possible , the name of an executive was supplied along with the company name and address . END
the forty-four lists supplied by the aia member companies were merged and duplicate names were eliminated . END
there was further elimination of all companies that were not accompanied by the name of a responsible company executive . END
the remaining names were then checked against the thomas register list ( see below ) and duplicate names were removed from the aia lists . END
by these steps the final aia list was reduced from 8,000 to UNK . END
the third list was selected by the research team on a random basis from the thomas register . END
it was compiled as a control sample to determine if the opinions and practices of companies on the lists submitted by the members of the aerospace industries association were materially different from those of other small firms selling to defense programs . END
such a difference might have resulted from : 1 . END
the fact that the aerospace industries association members whose lists were used did not comprise all firms engaged in defense programs . END
the fact that companies on the aia lists were already participating in the defense program because of the manner of their selection . END
accordingly , as `` in-group '' , they might have different opinions and practices than an `` UNK '' composed of those companies not so participating but interested in defense business . END
the fact that aia lists might not have been selected on a random basis . END
the control sample was selected by taking the bottom name of each of the two columns of names on each page of the alphabetical listing of manufacturers in the thomas register . END
if the bottom name in each column did not have a responsible executive identified , the next name above which identified such a responsible executive was substituted . END
fifteen hundred names were selected in this fashion . END
each questionnaire was mailed with a cover letter addressed personally to the president or other executive of each firm . END
the questionnaires were mailed in seattle , washington , and sent by regular mail to addresses in the states of UNK , UNK , oregon , and washington . END
airmail was used for the addresses outside the pacific northwest . END
each letter contained a UNK return envelope by regular mail for addresses in the pacific northwest , and by airmail for those outside the pacific northwest . END
approximately ten days after the questionnaire was mailed , a follow-up airmail postcard was sent to each of the original names . END
the first test mailing ( to 14 companies ) was made in june , 1960 . END
the second test mailing ( to 100 companies ) was made in july , 1960 . END
the final mailing of the questionnaire was made late in august , 1960 , to UNK firms consisting of UNK from the aia list and UNK from the tr list . END

the vast central valley of california is one of the most productive agricultural areas in the world . END
during the summer of 1960 , it became the setting for a bitter and basic labor-management struggle . END
the contestants in this economic struggle are the agricultural workers organizing committee ( awoc ) of the afl-cio and the agricultural employers of the state . END
by virtue of the legal responsibilities of the department of employment in the farm placement program , we necessarily found ourselves in the middle between these two forces . END
it is not a pleasant or easy position , but one we have UNK to maintain . END
we have sought to be strictly neutral as between the parties , but at the same time we have been required frequently to rule on specific issues or situations as they arose . END
inevitably , one side was pleased and the other displeased , regardless of how we ruled . END
often the displeased parties interpreted our decision as implying favoritism toward the other . END
we have UNK ourselves with the thought that this is a normal human reaction and is one of the consequences of any decision in an adversary proceeding . END
it is disconcerting , nevertheless , to read in a labor weekly , `` UNK knuckles down to UNK '' , and then to be confronted with a UNK publication which states , `` UNK recognizes obviously phony and UNK strikes as UNK UNK '' . END
for a number of years , there have been sporadic attempts in california to organize farm workers . END
these attempts met with little UNK for a variety of reasons . END
they were UNK financed , without experienced leadership , and lacked the general support of organized labor as a whole . END
this past year the pattern has been different : the organizing program had the full support of the afl-cio , which supplied staff and money to the awoc , as well as moral support . END
leadership was experienced and skillful , and financial resources were significant . END
regardless of where personal sympathies may lie as between the parties , failure to recognize these changed conditions would be to ignore the facts of life . END
as a result of these changed conditions , the impact of the organizational effort on agricultural labor-management relations has been much greater than in the past . END
the awoc has been able to employ the traditional weapons of labor -- the strike and the picket line -- with considerable success , particularly in the area of wages . END
by the very nature of the situation , it is the union which has been able to select the time and place to bring pressure upon management . END
to date , at least , the strategy of the awoc has been selective ; ; END
that is to say , to concentrate on a particular crop or activity in a particular area at a strategic time , rather than any UNK engagement with management throughout an area or the state . END
primarily , we became involved in these disputes because of our referral obligations under our farm placement program . END
normally , because agricultural labor is not covered by unemployment insurance , we would not expect any issues to arise regarding benefit payments under the trade dispute provision of the unemployment insurance code , although such a situation is quite within the realm of possibility . END
but the current issues arose out of the wagner-peyser act concerning referrals to an establishment where a labor dispute exists , and out of public law UNK and the UNK labor agreement if mexican nationals were employed at the ranch . END
most of us remember and think of the wagner-peyser act in its historical sense , as a major milestone in the development of public placement services . END
UNK do we think of it as a living , continuing , operating control over the system . END
however , when labor disputes arise , its provisions come clearly into play . END
california has accepted the provisions of that act ( as have all other states ) by enacting into our code ( section UNK ) a provision that END
the state of california accepts the provisions of the wagner-peyser act , and will observe and comply with the requirements of that act . END
with respect to labor disputes , the wagner-peyser act states only , END
in carrying out the provisions of this act , the secretary is authorized and directed to provide for the giving of notice of strikes or UNK to applicants before they are referred to employment . END
other provisions of the act UNK the secretary to adopt regulations necessary to carry out its provisions , and he has done so . END
the pertinent regulation for our purposes is section UNK ( , ) , as follows : END
referrals in labor dispute situations . END
no person shall be referred to a position the filling of which will aid directly or indirectly in filling a job which ( 1 ) is vacant because the former occupant is on strike or is being locked out in the course of a labor dispute , or ( 2 ) the filling of which is an issue in a labor dispute . END
with respect to positions not covered by UNK ( 1 ) or ( 2 ) of this paragraph , any individual may be referred to a place of employment in which a labor dispute exists , provided he is given written notice of such dispute prior to or at the time of his referral . END
in analyzing this regulation , let us take the last sentence first . END
it permits referrals under certain circumstances even when there is a labor dispute , provided the individual is given written notice of such a dispute . END
assume , for example , a situation where a farm has a packing shed and fields . END
the packing shed workers go on strike . END
there is no dispute involving UNK . END
we concluded that we may refer workers to the UNK ( but not the packing shed work ) provided we give them written notice of the packing shed dispute . END
so far , no troublesome cases have arisen under this provision . END
it is the first part of the regulation that is currently at issue . END
note that it UNK referrals under either condition ( 1 ) or condition ( 2 ) . END
employer representatives have contended that the secretary has gone beyond his authority by such a prohibition , on the grounds that the wagner-peyser act requires only written notice to the prospective worker that a dispute exists . END
the matter got into the courts this way : one of the early strikes called by the awoc was at the UNK pear orchards in UNK county . END
we found that a labor dispute existed , and that the workers had left their jobs , which were then vacant because of the dispute . END
accordingly , under clause ( 1 ) of the secretary's regulation , we suspended referrals to the employer . END
( incidentally , no mexican nationals were involved . END
) the employer , seeking to continue his harvest , challenged our right to cease referrals to him , and sought relief in the superior court of UNK county . END
the court issued a temporary restraining order , directing us to resume referrals . END
we , of course , obeyed the court order . END
however , the attorney general of california , at the request of the secretary of labor , sought to have the jurisdiction over the issue removed to the federal district court , on grounds that it was predominantly a federal issue since the validity of the secretary's regulation was being challenged . END
however , the federal court held that since the state had accepted the provisions of the wagner-peyser act into its own code , and presumably therefore also the regulations , it was now a state matter . END
it accordingly refused to assume jurisdiction , whereupon the california superior court made the restraining order permanent . END
under that order , we have continued referring workers to the ranch . END
a similar case arose at the UNK ranch in UNK county , and the superior court of that county issued similar restraining orders . END
the UNK have UNK argued that i should have accepted the superior court decisions as conclusive and issued statewide instructions to our staff to ignore this provision in the secretary's regulation . END
i cannot accept that view , either as a lawyer or as an administrator . END
first , let us examine briefly some of the legal considerations involved . END
it is an accepted UNK principle in california that a superior court decision does not constitute a binding legal precedent . END
it is conclusive , unless appealed , only upon the particular parties to the particular action which was heard . END
it is not binding upon another superior court , which could rule to the contrary . END
only when a decision is rendered by the district court of appeal ( or , of course , the supreme court ) is a binding precedent established . END
in that event , we can correctly say that we have received an authoritative interpretation of the matter , and one which we can follow statewide with confidence that the policy will not be UNK in other superior courts . END
but over and beyond the compelling need for a binding precedent decision , i am convinced that the decisions of the superior courts which in effect UNK the secretary's regulation are not a correct interpretation of the secretary's power under the federal law . END
i believe i am in good company in this view . END
the attorney general of california UNK in this interpretation and has filed an appeal from these decisions to the district court of appeal . END
the attorney general of the united states , in considering the power of the secretary to issue similar regulations under the wagner-peyser act relating to the interstate recruitment of farm workers , has rendered an opinion UNK his authority . END
further , and as an evidence of legislative intent only , the senate of the united states recently defeated by a substantial majority the `` holland amendment '' to the fair labor standards act , which would have specifically limited the regulatory authority of the secretary in these matters . END
next , let us consider briefly the program and administrative implications of a failure on our part to pursue our appeals . END
there is far too much at stake for all of the parties concerned to leave the matter hanging in UNK . END
the UNK of the issue are enormous . END
a decision to refer workers to jobs vacant because of a strike would have to be applied equally to UNK situations , and might in effect place the public employment services in the position of acting as UNK . END
the public interest is so dominant in such an issue that i cannot be so presumptuous as to attempt to settle it by an administrative order based upon conclusions reached in a summary action in one or two superior courts in the state . END
it is an issue which may well reach the supreme court of the united states before judicial finality is achieved . END
as an administrator , i cannot place the employment service in california in jeopardy of being out of compliance with the federal laws by my failure to pursue the avenues of appeal open to me . END
to have applied statewide the decisions of the two cases heard in superior court , in my opinion , would have placed us clearly out of compliance with the wagner-peyser act and would have immediately opened the way for the secretary of labor , were he so inclined , to notify the governor of such UNK , set a date for hearing , and issue his finding . END
the impact of UNK under the wagner-peyser act is clear : the withdrawal of some UNK million a year of administrative funds which finance our employment service program or , as a UNK , the taking over by the federal government of its operation . END
thus far , the cases which have come before the courts have involved only the issue of referral where the job is vacant due to a strike -- condition ( 1 ) in the regulation of the secretary . END
none has yet arisen under condition ( 2 ) , relating to referral to jobs `` the filling of which is an issue in a labor dispute '' . END
here the problem is essentially one of defining the word `` filling '' . END
should it be defined in a narrow sense to include only such elements as job specifications , union membership , union jurisdiction , and the like ? ? END
or should it have a broader connotation of including wage demands and other factors not necessarily associated with the mechanics of `` filling '' the job . END
because of the uncertainty of this definition , i UNK the interpretation of the secretary of labor . END
he has advised me that the narrower interpretation is the proper one ; ; END
that is , that if wages , for example , is the only issue in a labor dispute , and no workers have left their jobs because of the dispute , we may continue to make referrals . END
martin and UNK present evidence that UNK and young children can and do solve many problems at a relatively simple perceptual level simply by combining objects and counting them . END

many studies indicate that elementary-school children's interests cover the whole field of science ; ; END

how a child feels about himself , about other people , and about the tasks confronting him in school may have as much influence on his success in school as his physical and intellectual characteristics . END
a considerable amount of evidence exists to show that an unhappy and UNK child is not likely to do well in school subjects . END
emotional maturity is the result of many factors , the principal ones being the experiences of the first few years of the child's life . END
however , the teacher who understands the influence of emotions on behavior may be highly influential in helping pupils gain confidence , security , and satisfaction . END
concerning this responsibility of the teacher , suggestions for helping children gain better control of the emotions are presented in chapter 11 . END
the following generalizations about the emotional characteristics of elementary-school children may be helpful . END
typically , the young child's emotional reactions last for a relatively short time , as contrasted to those of an adult . END
as the child grows older , his emotional reactions lead to `` moods '' , or emotional states drawn out over a period of time and expressed slowly , rather than in short , abrupt outbursts . END
studies of the growth and decline of children's fears indicate that fears due to strange objects , noises , falling , and unexpected movement decline during the UNK years , but that fears of the dark , of being alone , and of imaginary creatures or robbers increase . END
UNK a child for being afraid or forcing him to meet the feared situation alone are poor ways of dealing with the problem ; ; END
more effective solutions include explanations , the example of another child , or conditioning by UNK the feared object , place , or person with something pleasant . END
children need help in learning to control their emotions . END
the young child learns from parents and teachers that temper UNK , screaming , kicking , and hitting will not get him what he wants ; ; END
the older child learns that intense emotional outbursts will not win approval by his peers , and , therefore , makes a real effort to control his emotions . END
children differ widely in their emotional responses . END
among UNK the patterns of emotional responses are similar ; ; END
as the influence of learning and environment are felt , emotional behavior becomes individualized . END
although no national norms exist for the social development of children , the teacher can find a great deal of information concerning types of social behavior normally displayed by children at various age levels . END
the following summary will give the student some idea about the social characteristics of elementary-school children ; ; END
the student will certainly want to explore more deeply into the fascinating study of immature individuals , struggling to meet their developmental needs , and at the same time trying to learn the rules of the game in the UNK number of groups in which they hold membership . END
during early childhood , children are more interested in the approval of their parents and teachers than they are in the approval of other children ; ; END
after they have been in school a few years , their interest in UNK of their own age increases , and their interest in adults decreases ; ; END
the child who had once considered it a treat to accompany his parents on UNK and family gatherings now considers it a bore . END
in late childhood the influence of the group on the social behavior of the child continues to increase ; ; END
the group sets the styles in clothing , the kind of play engaged in , and the ideals of right and wrong behavior . END
in early childhood the choice of a companion is likely to be for another child of his own age or a year or two older , who can do the things he likes to do ; ; END
such factors as sex , intelligence , and status in the group do not influence his choice much at this time . END
in later childhood , an interest in team games replaces individual play ; ; END
loyalty to the group , a feeling of superiority over those who are not members , and unwillingness to play with members of the opposite sex become dominant traits . END
during early childhood boys tease and bully , on the average , more than girls ; ; END
those who feel inferior or UNK engage in these activities more than do UNK children . END
during late childhood boys like to tease , UNK , and talk smart to girls ; ; END
girls , who are more mature than boys , UNK upon the youthful antics of boys of their own age . END
by the time pupils reach the sixth grade , their ethical and moral standards are fairly well developed ; ; END
they exhibit a keen interest in social , political , and economic problems , but they frequently have vague and incorrect notions about the terms they use rather glibly in their routine school work . END
between the ages of two and four years , UNK or resistance to adult authority is noticeable ; ; END
after the fourth year it begins to decline . END
however , as we have seen , in later childhood the child begins to substitute the standards of the peer group for those of parents and teachers . END
the elementary-school child grows gradually in his ability to work in groups . END
the child in the primary grades can play UNK with one companion , but his desire to be first in everything gets him into trouble when the group gets larger ; ; END
he wants to be with people , but he hasn't yet learned to cooperate . END
in the middle grades , however , he begins to participate more effectively in group activities such as selecting a leader , helping to make plans and carry on group activities , and setting up rules governing the enterprise . END
why the teacher should study the individual pupil END
much progress has been made in the last two decades in developing techniques for understanding children , yet in almost any classroom today can be found children whose needs are not being met by the school program . END
some are failing to achieve as much as their ability would permit ; ; END
others never seem able to enter fully into the life of the classroom . END
these children have been described as those who were trying to say something to adults who did not understand . END
many school systems now employ school psychologists and child guidance specialists . END
these specialists perform valuable services by helping teachers learn to identify children who need special attention , by suggesting ways of meeting the needs of individual children in the regular classroom , and by providing clinical services for severely UNK children . END
it is the classroom teacher , however , who has daily contacts with pupils , and who is in a unique position to put sound psychological principles into practice . END
indeed , a study of the individual child is an integral part of the work of the elementary-school teacher , rather than merely an additional chore . END
teachers and administrators in many elementary schools have assumed that dividing the pupils in any grade into groups on the basis of test scores UNK the problem of meeting the needs of individuals . END
what they should recognize is that children who have been placed in one of these groups on a narrow academic basis still differ widely in attributes that influence success , and that they still must be treated as individuals . END
although the teacher must be concerned with maintaining standards , he must also be concerned about understanding differences in ability , background , and experience . END
factors that inhibit learning and lead to maladjustment END
studies conducted in various sections of the united states indicate that many children in elementary schools are UNK emotionally , and that many of them are failing to make satisfactory progress in school subjects . END
one study , which involved UNK pupils in grades one to six , found that 12 percent of the pupils were seriously UNK and that 23 percent were reading a year below capacity . END
it is apparent , therefore , that the teacher needs to know what factors have a vital bearing on the learning and adjustment of children . END
when a child fails to meet the standards of the school in his rate of learning , insecurity , unhappiness , and other forms of maladjustment frequently follow . END
these UNK in turn inhibit learning , and a vicious cycle is completed . END
it is easy for the teacher to rationalize that the child who is not achieving in accordance with his known ability is just plain lazy , or that the child who lacks interest in school , who dislikes the teacher , or who is UNK is a hopeless delinquent . END
the causes of UNK and maladjustment may be found in physical factors , such as defective speech or hearing , impaired vision , faulty motor coordination , a frail constitution , chronic disease , malnutrition , and UNK UNK . END
they may be caused by poor health habits , such as faulty eating and sleeping habits . END
they may be related to mental UNK or lack of UNK for certain types of school work . END
the curriculum may be too difficult for some and too easy for others . END
teaching methods , learning materials , and promotion policies may inhibit learning and lead to UNK for some children . END
UNK family relations , broken homes , and undesirable community influences may also be contributing factors . END
this is only a minimum list of the factors that inhibit learning and contribute to maladjustment among children . END
moreover , these conditions do not influence all children in the same manner . END
a vision handicap that may produce nervous tension and reading disability for one child may spur another child on to even greater achievement in reading . END
an UNK home that may discourage one child may constitute the motivation causing another to work harder for successful achievement in school . END
at any rate , the teacher who recognizes common causes of UNK and maladjustment can frequently do a great deal to eliminate the causes of pupil UNK , failure , and maladjustment . END
sources of information about children END
successful teaching involves getting enough information about each pupil to understand why he UNK as he does in certain situations and how his achievement in school is being influenced by various factors in his environment . END
the classroom teacher cannot be expected to be as proficient in the use of the techniques of child study as the clinical psychologist ; ; END
he cannot be expected to UNK all the tests and gather all the information needed about each child in his classroom . END
he can be expected , however , to examine and interpret the information already available ; ; END
to UNK and extend his own techniques for studying individual children ; ; END
and to utilize opportunities , arising in connection with regular classroom activities , for gaining a better understanding of his pupils . END
this section deals with some of the sources of information that can be tapped by the classroom teacher ; ; END
chapter 15 provides more detailed information about specific techniques used in evaluating pupil progress . END
most school systems today maintain a system of cumulative records of pupils . END
these records , when systematically maintained , provide much information about the children , which the teacher can use in guidance , instruction , grouping , and reporting to parents . END
each teacher has in his classroom a metal file , equipped with a lock , which is used to store cumulative record UNK . END
during summer vacation periods these records are stored in the office of the principal . END
only the teacher and other professional personnel are permitted to see or use these records . END
each new teacher to whom the pupil goes is expected to study the information in the cumulative record and to bring it up to date . END
some school systems provide written instructions to principals and teachers UNK when certain information is to be recorded on cumulative record forms and explaining how the information is to be summarized and used . END

from the summary report before us at this conference , a number of problems are apparent . END

in order to simplify the exposition of a typical fully apportioned cost analysis , let us assume the application of the analysis to an electric utility company supplying a single city with power generated by its own UNK plant . END

for a statement of costs per kilowatt-hour would ignore the fact that many of these costs are not a function of kilowatt-hour output ( or consumption ) of energy . END

per kilowatt-hour , and a $2 charge for a maximum demand of 2 UNK during the month at the rate of $1 per UNK -- a total bill of UNK for that month . END

costs actually vary in much more complex ways . END
these are those operating and capital costs found to vary with number of customers regardless , or almost regardless , of power consumption . END

i hold , on the contrary , that we mean to assert something of the pain itself , namely , that it was bad -- bad when and as it occurred . END
consider what follows from the positivist view . END
on that view , nothing good or bad happened in the case until i came on the scene and made my remark . END
for what i express in my remark is something going on in me at the time , and that of course did not exist until i did come on the scene . END

badness , in the only sense in which it is involved at all , waited for its appearance till i came and looked and felt . END
now that this is at odds with our meaning may be shown as follows . END
let us put to ourselves the hypothesis that we had not come on the scene and that the rabbit never was discovered . END
are we prepared to say that in that case nothing bad occurred in the sense in which we said it did ? ? END
indeed , we should say , on the contrary , that the accident of our later discovery made no difference whatever to the badness of the UNK pain , that it would have been every UNK as bad whether a chance UNK happened later to discover the body and feel UNK or not . END

we are saying that the pain was bad when and as it occurred and before anyone took an attitude toward it . END
the first argument is thus an ideal experiment in which we use the method of difference . END
it removes our present expression and shows that the badness we meant would not be affected by this , whereas on positivist grounds it should be . END
the second argument applies the method in the reverse way . END
it ideally removes the past event , and shows that this would render false what we mean to say , whereas on positivist grounds it should not . END
let us suppose that the animal did not in fact fall into the trap and did not suffer at all , but that we UNK believe it did , and say as before that its suffering was an evil thing . END
on the positivist theory , everything i sought to express by calling it evil in the first case is still present in the second . END
in the only sense in which badness is involved at all , whatever was bad in the first case is still present in its entirety , since all that is expressed in either case is a state of feeling , and that feeling is still there . END
and our question is , is such an implication consistent with what we meant ? ? END
clearly it is not . END
if anyone asked us , after we made the remark that the suffering was a bad thing , whether we should think it relevant to what we said to learn that the incident had never occurred and no pain had been suffered at all , we should say that it made all the difference in the world , that what we were asserting to be bad was precisely the suffering we thought had occurred back there , that if this had not occurred , there was nothing left to be bad , and that our assertion was in that case mistaken . it overlooks the other fact that it is described as `` UNK minus UNK UNK UNK '' , `` no less salutary than gay '' . END
it also overlooks the fact that in a rational UNK , and quite clearly in more's UNK , the opposite of serious is not gay but frivolous , and the opposite of gay is not serious but solemn . END
more believed that a man could be both serious and gay . END
that a writer who is gay cannot be serious is a common professional illusion , UNK fostered by all too many UNK who UNK believe that their frivolous efforts should be taken seriously because they are expressed with that dreary UNK which is the only mode of expression their authors are capable of . utopia is still widely read because in a sense more stood on the margin of modernity . END

there are more substantial criticisms to be made of fromm's account of capitalist civilization . END
it is worthwhile to recall that fromm's treatment has both descriptive and UNK aspects . END
since i have already discussed his moral position , that discussion is incorporated by reference into the following pages , which will focus on the empirical and analytic side of fromm's treatment . END
i shall first indicate a couple of weaknesses in fromm's analysis , then argue that , granted these weaknesses , he still has much left that is valuable , and , finally , raise the general question of a philosophical versus a sociological approach to the question of alienation . END
almost no empirical work has been done on the problem of alienation . END
despite its rather long intellectual history , alienation is still a promising hypothesis and not a verified theory . END
the idea has received much attention in philosophy , in literature , and in a few works of general social criticism , such as the sane society . END
what is missing is work that would answer , presumably by the use of survey methods and UNK attitude scales , such questions as these : what are the components of the UNK described as alienation ? ? END
how widespread is alienation ? ? END
what is its incidence among the various classes and subgroups of the population ? ? END
taking alienation as a dependent variable , with what UNK factors is it most highly associated ? ? END
considered as an independent variable , how does it affect behavior in various sectors of life ? ? END
until such work is done , there must remain the nagging suspicion that alienation may be little more than an expression of the malaise of the intellectual , who , rejected by and in turn rejecting the larger society , projects his own fear and despair onto the broader social screen . END
i am not suggesting that fromm ought to do this kind of work . END
nor do i think that alienation is nothing more than a projection of the malaise of the intellectual . END
i am saying only that until a fuller and different kind of evidence comes in , any discussion of alienation must be understood to have certain important limitations . END
until such evidence appears , we must make do with the evidence we have . END
here , perhaps , fromm is vulnerable , for he does not always use the best and most recent evidence available , and he sometimes selects and UNK the evidence in rather special ways . END
fromm's analysis of alienation in the sphere of production centers around the concepts of the UNK of the corporation , the separation of ownership from control , and the broad ( and thus from the point of view of corporate control , UNK ) UNK of stock ownership . END
for all these points he relies exclusively on berle and UNK study of UNK , the modern corporation and private property . END
the broad conclusions of that UNK work remain UNK , but subsequent research has expanded and somewhat altered their empirical support , has suggested important revisions in the general analytic frame of reference , and has sharpened the meaning of particular analytic concepts in this area . END
fromm seems unaware of these developments . END
another example is his very infrequent use of the large amount of data from surveys designed to discover what and how people actually do feel and think on a broad range of topics : he cites such UNK findings just three times . END
moreover , the conclusions he draws from the findings are not always the only ones possible . END
for example , he cites the following data from two studies on job satisfaction : in the first study , 85 per cent of professionals and executives , UNK per cent of white collar people , and 41 per cent of factory workers expressed satisfaction with their jobs ; ; END
in the second study , the percentages were 86 for professionals , 74 for managerial persons , 42 for commercial employees , 56 for skilled workers , and 48 for UNK workers . END
he concludes that these data show a `` remarkably high '' percentage of consciously dissatisfied and unhappy persons among factory and clerical workers . END
starting from other value premises than fromm's , some analysts might conclude that the percentages really tell us very little at all , while others might even conclude that the figures are remarkably low . END
UNK UNK , for example , once said that america was a paradise -- the only one in the history of the world -- for UNK and small children . END
what matters is that while fromm's reading of the data is not the only one possible , it is precisely the one we would expect from a writer who earnestly believes that every man can and ought to be happy and satisfied . END
fromm also cites a poll on attitudes toward work restriction conducted by the opinion research corporation in 1945 , in which UNK per cent of manual workers said a man ought to turn out as much as he could in a day's work , while 41 per cent said he should not do his best but should turn out only the average amount . END
fromm says these data show that job dissatisfaction and resentment are widespread . END
that is one way to read the findings , but again there are other ways . END
one might use such findings to indicate the strength of informal primary associations in the factory , an interpretation which would run counter to fromm's theory of alienation . END
or , he might remind fromm that the 41 per cent figure is really astonishingly low : after all , the medieval guild system was dedicated to the proposition that 100 per cent of the workers ought to turn out only the average amount ; ; END
and today's trade unions announce pretty much the same view . END
in view of these shortcomings in both the amount and the interpretation of UNK findings on public opinion , and considering the criticisms which can be brought against fromm's philosophical anthropology , such a passage as the following cannot be taken seriously . END
`` are people happy , are they as satisfied , unconsciously , as they believe themselves to be ? ? END
considering the nature of man , and the conditions for happiness , this can hardly be so '' . END
the ambiguities suggested above stem from a more basic difficulty in fromm's style of thought . END
he seems to use the term alienation in two different ways . END
sometimes he uses it as a subjective , descriptive term , and sometimes as an objective , diagnostic one . END

when such a reference is found , an actual address is assigned and the availability table is changed so that the assigned index word or switch is no longer available for later assignment . END
if the one- or two-digit address of an index word or electronic switch is used or is included in the operand of an UNK or UNK statement ( see page 99 ) , the corresponding index word or electronic switch is reserved . END
if a statement has been assigned an address in the index word area END
by means of an actual label or END
by means of an origin statement which refers to an actual address , the corresponding index word will be reserved . END
these entries should normally appear at the beginning of the program or immediately following each UNK statement . END
otherwise , symbolic names may have previously been assigned to these same index words . END
( this method does not apply to electronic switches . END
the preceding methods allow efficient use of index words and electronic switches during a UNK or UNK program , particularly when used in conjunction with the UNK statement . END
extreme caution should be used , however , to avoid the conflicting usage of an index word or electronic switch which may result from the assignment of more than one name or function to the same address . END
if the symbolic name or actual address of an index word or electronic switch appears or is included in the operand of an UNK or UNK statement ( see page UNK ) , the specified index word or electronic switch will again be made available , regardless of the method by which it was reserved . END
it will not , however , be used for symbolic assignment until all other index words or electronic switches have been assigned for the first time . END
if , at any time during the assignment pass , the compiler finds that there are no more index words available for assignment , the warning message `` no more index words available '' will be placed in the object program listing , the table will be altered to show that index words 1 through 96 are available , and the assignment will continue as before . END
if the compiler finds that there are no more electronic switches available for assignment , the warning message `` no more electronic switches available '' will be placed in the object program listing , the table will be altered to show that electronic switches 1 through 30 are available , and assignment will continue as before . END
the resultant conflicting usage of index words or electronic switches may be avoided by reducing the number of symbolic names used , e.g. , through the proper use of the equ , UNK , or UNK statements . END
as noted in appendix c , index words 97 through 99 are never available for assignment to symbolic names by the compiler ; ; END
also , index words UNK through 96 may have been made unavailable for assignment . END
autocoder declarative statements provide the processor with the necessary information to complete the imperative operations properly . END
declarative statements are never executed in the object program and should be separated from the program instruction area , placed preferably at its beginning or end . END
otherwise , special care must be taken to branch around them so that the program will not attempt to execute something in a data area as an instruction . END
if the compiler does encounter such statements , a warning message will be issued . END
7070/7074 autocoder includes the following declarative statements : da ( define area ) , dc ( define constant ) , UNK ( define record definition word ) , UNK ( define switch ) , UNK ( define line ) , equ ( equate ) , UNK ( define tape file ) , diocs ( define input/output control system ) , and duf ( descriptive entry for unit records ) . END
da , dc , UNK , and UNK require more than one entry . END
the da statement is used to name and define the positions and length of fields within an area . END
the dc statement is used to name and enter constants into the object program . END
since the UNK and UNK make use of record definition words ( rdws ) to read , write , move , and otherwise examine blocks of storage , the da and dc statements provide the option of generating rdws automatically . END
when so instructed , autocoder will generate one or more rdws and assign them successive locations immediately preceding the area with which they are to be associated . END
an UNK will be of the form af , where UNK is the starting location of the area and UNK is its ending location . END
these addresses are calculated automatically by the processor . END
in some cases , it may be more advantageous to assign locations to rdws associated with da and dc areas in some other part of storage , i.e. , not immediately preceding the da or dc areas . END
the UNK statement may be used for this purpose . END
the UNK statement may also be used to generate an UNK defining any area specified by the UNK . END
as many as ten digital switches may be named and provided by the UNK statement for consideration by the UNK and logic UNK . END
each switch occupies one UNK position in a word , can be set on or off , and is considered as logically equivalent to an electronic switch . END
it cannot , however , be referred to by electronic switch commands , e.g. , UNK , UNK , etc. . END
an individual switch or the entire set of switches in a word may be tested or altered as desired . END
through use of the UNK statement , a means is provided for UNK both the editing of fields to be inserted in a print line area and the layout of the area itself . END
the area may include constant information supplied by the UNK . END
the area may also be provided with additional data during the running of the object program by means of UNK or move UNK . END
the declarative statement equ permits the UNK to equate symbolic names to actual index words , electronic switches , arm and file numbers , tape channel and unit numbers , alteration switches , etc. , and to equate a symbol to another symbol or to an actual address . END
the diocs , UNK , and duf statements are used when required by the input/output control system . END
diocs is used to select the major methods of processing to be used , and to name the index words used by aj . END
each tape file must be described by tape file specifications , produced by aj . END
in addition to information related to the file and its records , the file specifications contain UNK locations and the location of tape label information . END
a duf entry must be supplied for every unit record file describing the type of file and the unit record equipment to be used . END
the duf also supplies the locations of UNK written by the user that are unique to the file . END
a full description of the diocs , UNK , and duf statements is contained in the UNK data processing system bulletin `` ibm UNK input/output control system '' , form aj . END
