thus , the three main categories of antisubmarine warfare operations are defense of shipping , defense of naval forces , and area defense . END
the last category UNK the others in UNK operations and near terminals and bases . END
to effect these operations , five elements exist ( 1 ) surface , ( 2 ) air , ( 3 ) mines , ( 4 ) submarine , and ( 5 ) fixed installations . END
surface forces have been used to provide defense UNK around naval and merchant ship formations , air to furnish area surveillance , and mines for protection of limited areas . END
submarines and shore installations are new elements . END
the submarine now has a definite place in submarine defense particularly in denying enemy access to ocean areas . END
fixed installations offer possibilities for area detection . END
mine warfare is being UNK against submarine targets . END
a sixth element , not always considered , is intelligence . END
it includes operational intelligence of the enemy and knowledge of the environment . END
operational intelligence presumably will be available from our national intelligence agencies ; ; END
intelligence on the environment will come from the recently augmented program in UNK . END
the major postwar development is the certainty that these elements should not be considered singly but in combination and as being mutually supporting . END
necessity for an over-all concept END
thinking on submarine defense has not always been clear-cut . END
proponents of single elements tend to ensure UNK of that element without determining if it is justified , and the element with the most enthusiastic and UNK proponents has assumed the greatest importance . END
consequently , air , surface , and submarine elements UNK the mine , fixed installations , and intelligence . END
these have sought more and more of what they have . END
each seems to strive for elimination of the necessity for the others . END
this , despite postwar experience demonstrating that all elements are necessarily mutually supporting . END
thus , the most productive areas are not necessarily the most stressed . END
this is stated to emphasize the necessity for an over-all concept of submarine defense , one which would provide positions of relative importance to UNK elements based on projected potentialities . END
then the enthusiasm and energy of all elements can be UNK to produce cumulative progress toward a common objective . END
an over-all concept would have other advantages . END
it would allow presentation to the public of a unified approach . END
now the problem is presented UNK and sometimes UNK . END
while one element is announcing progress , another is UNK its problems . END
the result can only be confusion in the public mind . END
a unified concept can serve as a guide to budgeting and , if public support is gained , will command congressional support . END
industry's main criticism of the navy's antisubmarine effort is that it cannot determine where any one company or industry can apply its skills and know-how . END
lacking guidance , industry picks its own areas . END
the result , coupled with the salesmanship for which american industry is famous , is considerable expenditure of funds and efforts in marginal areas . END
an over-all concept will guide industry where available talents and facilities will yield greatest dividends . END
therefore , a broad concept of over-all submarine defense is needed for UNK of the navy's efforts , for a logical presentation to the public , for industry's guidance , and as a basis for a program to the congress . END
principles involved in an over-all concept END
that which follows will be a discussion of principles and possible content for an over-all concept of antisubmarine warfare . END
russia possesses the UNK of submarines in the world , divided between her various UNK . END
some are also in UNK and others are on loan to egypt . END
other countries which may willingly or UNK become communist can furnish bases . END
communist target areas can be assumed , but there is no certainty that such assumptions coincide with soviet intentions . END
attack can come from almost any direction against many locations . END
logically , then , the first principle of the plan must be that it is not rigidly oriented toward any geographical area . END
it is often stated that the submarine can be destroyed while building , at bases , in transit , and on station . END
destruction of the enemy's building and base complex , however , requires attacks on enemy territory , which is possible only in event of all-out hostilities . END
in transit or on station , it may not be possible to attack the submarines until commission of an overt act . END
the communists are adept at utilizing hostilities short of general war and will do so whenever it is to their advantage . END
therefore the second principle of the plan must be that , while providing for all-out hostilities , its effectiveness is not dependent on general war . END
antisubmarine warfare does not involve UNK between large opposing forces , with the decision a result of a single battle . END
it is a war of attrition , of single actions , of an exchange of losses . END
this exchange must result in our ending up with some effective units . END
initially , having fewer units of some elements -- especially submarines -- than the opponent , our capabilities need to be sufficiently greater than theirs , so that the exchange will be in our favor . END
therefore , the third principle of the plan must be that it does not depend for effectiveness on engagement by the same types , unless at an assured favorable exchange rate . END
the submarine has increased its effectiveness by several orders of magnitude since world war 2 . END
its speed has increased , it operates at increasingly greater depths , its submerged endurance is becoming unlimited , and it will become even more silent . END
the next developments will probably be in UNK . END
the missile can gradually be expected to replace the UNK . END
as detection ranges increase , weapons will be developed to attack other submarines and surface craft at these ranges . END
therefore , the fourth principle of the plan must be that it provide for continuously increasing capabilities in the opponent . END
no element can accomplish the total objective of submarine defense . END
some elements support the others , but all have limitations . END
some limitations of one element can be compensated for by a capability of another . END
elements used in combination will increase the over-all capability more than the sum of the capabilities of the individual elements . END
therefore , the UNK fifth principle must be that it capitalize on the capabilities of all elements in combination . END
conceivably the submarine defense problem can be solved by sufficient forces . END
numbers would be astronomical and current fiscal policies make this an impractical solution . END
UNK , aircraft procurement , and weapon programs indicate that there will not be enough of anything . END
therefore , any measures taken in peacetime which will decrease force requirements in war will contribute greatly to success when hostilities occur . END
therefore , the sixth principle of the plan must be that it concentrate on current measures which will reduce future force requirements . END
the world is constantly changing ; ; END
what was new yesterday is UNK today . END
the seventh principle of the plan is self-evident ; ; END
it must be flexible enough to allow for technological UNK , scientific progress , and changes in world conditions . END
supporting elements in UNK operations END
to this point the need for an over-all plan for submarine defense has been demonstrated , the mission has been stated , broad principles UNK its content laid down , and the supporting elements listed . END
before considering these elements in more detail , an additional requirement should be stated . END
large area coverage will accomplish all other tasks . END
therefore , because reduction in tasks results in reduction of forces required , the plan should provide for expanding area coverage . END
but it must be remembered that the plan should not be oriented geographically . END
consequently , the system giving area coverage ( if such coverage is less than world wide ) must be flexible and hence at least partially mobile . END
since effective area coverage appears fairly remote , the requirement can be borne in mind while considering the elements : air , surface , UNK , fixed installations , mines , and intelligence . END
these are arranged approximately in the order of the UNK of their proponents but will be discussed in the reverse order in the hope that the true order of importance will result . END
intelligence , as used UNK , will include information on possible opponents and on the environment which can affect operations . END
these can be referred to as operational intelligence and environmental intelligence . END
in submarine defense these must have maximum stress . END
good operational intelligence can ensure sound planning , greatly reduce force requirements , and increase tactical effectiveness . END
environmental intelligence is just as important . END
the ocean presently UNK with the target . END
full knowledge of the science of UNK can bring the environment to our side , resulting in an increase in effectiveness of equipment and tactics , a decrease in enemy capabilities , and the development of methods of UNK on the environment . END
therefore , improved intelligence will result in reduced force requirements and , as it supports all other elements , rates a top priority . END
gathering intelligence is important , but of equal importance is its translation into usable form . END
a program is needed to translate the results of UNK research into tactical and operating instructions . END
approaching this problem on a statistical basis is invalid , because the opponent has the same sources available and will be encountered not under average conditions , but under the conditions most advantageous to him . END
therefore , the UNK commander must have detailed operating instructions based on measurement of conditions , in the area , at the time of encounter . END
all capabilities must be used to maximum advantage then . END
temperature , wind , oxygen content , depth , bottom character , and animal life are the chief environmental variables . END
there may be others . END
variations in sound velocity should be measured rather than temperature , because more of the variables would be UNK . END
these variations must eventually be measured UNK as well as UNK . END
progress in predicting water conditions is encouraging , but little guidance is available to the man at sea on the use of such information . END
a concurrent effort is needed to make UNK data useful on the spot . END
mine warfare has in the past been directed against surface targets . END
by its nature it has always been of great psychological advantage and small efforts have required considerably greater UNK . END
mines are being increasingly oriented against submarine targets . END
they are still considered to be for use in restricted waters , however , and targets must come within a few yards of them . END
mines need to be recognized as a major element in UNK warfare employment , extended to deep water , and have their effective area per unit increased . END
mines can be used to deny access to great areas ; ; END
they are difficult to counter , cost little to maintain until required , and can be put into place quickly . END
a most attractive feature is that detection and attack are combined in a single package . END
effective employment will reduce force requirements . END
for example , effective mine barriers from florida to cuba and across the UNK channel from cuba to mexico would remove all requirements for harbor defense , UNK patrol , UNK escort , shipping control , and mine defense for the entire gulf of mexico . END
more extended systems , covering all passage into the caribbean , would free the caribbean and the gulf of mexico from the previously listed requirements . END
systems covering the gulf of st. lawrence and possibly the entire coasts of the united states are not impossible . END
such mine defense systems could permit concentration of mobile forces in the open UNK with consequent increase in the probability of success . END
the advantages inherent in mine warfare justify as great an importance for this element as is accorded any of the other elements . END
fixed installations are increasingly advocated as the problem of area defense emerges . END
the proponents are scientific and technical men who exercise considerable influence on their military counterparts . END
systems which detect submarines over wide areas are attractive , although they can be only `` UNK UNK '' . END
mobile forces are required to UNK and attack detected targets , since the systems are not capable of UNK a target . END
such systems are expensive and are oriented geographically . END
in an over-all UNK concept , dependence on and effort expended for such systems should be limited to those with proven capabilities . END
no general installation should be made until a model installation has been proved and its maximum capability determined . END
in addition , proposals for fixed installations should be carefully weighed against a counterpart mobile system . END
fixed installations will always lack the flexibility that should be inherent in naval systems . END
the submarine has become increasingly attractive as an antisubmarine weapon system . END
it operates in its UNK environment , and any advantage gained therefrom by the target is shared by the attacker . END
but the submarine is a weapon of ambush and therefore always in danger of being UNK . END

because individual classes of foods differ in their requirements for preservation , a number of methods have been developed over the years involving one or a combination of procedures such as UNK , UNK , UNK , chemical treatment , canning , refrigeration , and freezing . END
the basic objectives in each instance are to make available supplies of food during the intervals between UNK or slaughter , to minimize losses resulting from the action of microorganisms and insects , and to make it possible to transport foods from the area of harvest or production to areas of consumption . END
in earlier years , the preservation of food was essentially related to survival . END
in the more sophisticated atmosphere of today's developed nations , food-preservation techniques have sought also to bring variety , peak freshness , and optimum taste and flavor in foods at reasonable cost to the consumer . END
with the development of nuclear technology , UNK materials , and machine radiation sources in recent years , the possibilities of applying ionizing radiation to the preservation of foods attracted the attention of investigators in the united states and throughout the world . END
an early hope that irradiation might be the ultimate answer to practically all food preservation problems was soon dispelled . END
interest remained , however , in the possibility that it would serve as a useful supplementary method for UNK spoilage losses and for preserving some foods at lower over-all costs than freezing , or without employing heat or chemicals with their attendant taste alterations . END
factors responsible for the spoilage of foods END
the chief factors responsible for the spoilage of fresh UNK are ( 1 ) microorganisms such as bacteria , molds , and UNK , ( 2 ) enzymes , ( 3 ) insects , ( 4 ) sprouting , and ( 5 ) chemical reactions . END
microorganisms are often responsible for the rapid spoilage of foods . END
of special concern is the growth of bacteria such as UNK UNK which generate poisonous products . END
enzymatic action in stored food produces changes which can UNK affect the appearance of food or its palatability . END
spoilage by chemical action results from the reaction of one group of components in the food with others or with its environment , as in corrosion of the walls of metal containers or the reaction of fats with oxygen in the air to produce UNK . END
sprouting is a naturally occurring phenomenon in stored potatoes , onions , carrots , UNK , and similar root vegetables . END
insect UNK is a problem of importance chiefly in stored grain . END
the presence of UNK organisms such as UNK UNK in pork introduces another factor which must be dealt with in food processing . END
to permit the storage of food for long periods of time , a method of preservation must accomplish the destruction of microorganisms and inhibition of enzymatic action . END
the term `` sterilization '' applies to methods involving essentially complete destruction of all microorganisms . END
food treated in this manner and protected from UNK by UNK methods of packaging and UNK presumably could be stored for long periods without refrigeration . END
the process of `` UNK '' involves UNK and less prolonged heat treatment which UNK the destruction of most , but not all , of the microorganisms . END
less severe thermal treatment as by blanching or UNK serves to UNK enzymes . END
general effects of ionizing radiation END
ionizing radiation can cause the destruction of microorganisms and insects involved in food spoilage or , at lower doses , can inhibit their action . END
it furnishes a means of destroying insects in stored grain products as well as certain UNK organisms present in meats . END
UNK of enzymes is also possible , although some types require extremely heavy doses of 10 UNK or more . END
because of undesirable UNK , odors , colors , and generally low palatability associated with radiation treatment of this magnitude , the UNK of enzymes is best accomplished prior to irradiation by the conventional UNK methods of blanching . END
radiation does not UNK the chemical spoilage of food . END
it will , however , inhibit the sprouting of potatoes and other root vegetables . END
the radiation doses required for the preservation of foods are in the following ranges : 1 . END
for radiosterilization , to destroy all organisms for long-term preservation -- about UNK UNK for UNK foods of low salt content . END
for radiopasteurization , to partially destroy microorganisms ; ; END
results vary with types of food , storage conditions , and objectives of treatment -- commonly of the order of 0.2 UNK but up to about 0.8 aj . END
for destruction of insects -- about 25,000 aj . END
for UNK the sprouting of root vegetables -- UNK to 10,000 aj . END
preserving foods with ionizing radiation leads to some undesirable side effects , particularly at the higher radiation dosages . END
in this respect , the general palatability and individual acceptance of most UNK foods has , to date , been found to be low in comparison with fresh and commercially processed foods . END
a number of foods are quite acceptable as regards taste and palatability , however , at dosages substantially less than sterilization levels . END
moreover , the UNK value of irradiated foods apparently UNK little , if any , change , although some of the UNK vitamins are affected by sterilization doses . END
for irradiation of food , the results obtained depend upon the dose rather than the specific type of radiation , and x-ray , gamma , and high-energy electron radiation are suitable . END
aside from availability and economic considerations , each has certain practical advantages ; ; END
for example , gamma rays give deeper penetration but cannot be focused or UNK , whereas UNK electron beams may be split and directed to both the top and bottom of the food package to be irradiated . END
selection of a source for commercial irradiation would involve consideration of numerous factors including required dose rate , load factor , UNK , convenience , safety , and most important , costs . END
of the potentially useful sources of ionizing UNK , gamma sources , UNK , UNK , fission products , or a reactor irradiation loop system using a material such as an UNK salt have received most attention for food-preservation systems . END
of the various particle UNK , the van de UNK machines , resonant UNK , and linear UNK are the principal ones available for commercial use . END
costs of the effective energy produced by these sources is a major obstacle in the development of food-preservation processes . END

conventional energy for processing foods is available in the range of at most a few cents per UNK for electric power and the equivalent of a few mills per UNK for process steam . END
radiation , therefore , is at an initial cost disadvantage even though only 1 to 10 per cent as much radiation energy as heat energy is required for radiopasteurization or radiosterilization . END
what are the possibilities of lowered radiation production costs for the future ? ? END

present status of irradiation preservation of foods END
the objective of complete sterilization of foods is to produce a wholesome and UNK product capable of being stored without refrigeration for extended periods of time . END
chief interest in radiosterilization resides in the military services . END
for them , providing UNK food under battle or emergency conditions is a paramount consideration . END
they require completely sterile foods capable of being stored without refrigeration , preferably items already cooked and ready to eat . END
high UNK value , variety , palatability , and UNK appearance are important for reasons of morale . END
foods for rear stations , which require cooking , but no refrigeration , are also of interest . END
of primary interest are meats . END
radiopasteurization , which produces fewer adverse sensory changes in food products , has potential usefulness in UNK the keeping qualities of fresh and refrigerated food items . END
thus , food so processed might reach more remote markets and permit the consumer to enjoy more produce at peak freshness and palatability . END
commercial interest is chiefly in this type of treatment , as is military interest under peacetime conditions . END
the present status of food preservation by ionizing radiation is discussed by food classes in the following paragraphs . END
the radiation processing of meat has received extensive investigation . END
to date , the one meat showing favorable results at sterilization doses is pork . END
of particular interest to the military services is the demonstration that roast pork , after radiosterilization , is superior in palatability to available canned pork products . END
tests with beef have been largely unsuccessful because of the development of UNK . END
a prime objective of the army UNK corps program is to find the reasons for UNK low palatability and means of overcoming it , since it is a major and desirable dietary item . END
partly because UNK heat treatment is needed to UNK enzymes before radiosterilization , treated fresh meats have the appearance of boiled or canned meat . END
UNK is a less severe problem with the radiopasteurization of meats , but problems of commercial UNK remain . END
moderate radiation doses of from 100,000 to 200,000 UNK can extend the shelf life ( at 35 f ) of fresh beef from 5 days to 5 or 6 weeks . END
however , the problem of consumer UNK remains . END
the UNK blanching process UNK the treated beef and liquid UNK in UNK cuts . END
cooked beef irradiated in the absence of oxygen assumes an unnatural pink color . END
when lamb and mutton are irradiated at UNK doses , the meat becomes UNK , the fat becomes UNK , and , again , unnatural changes in color occur . END
ground meats such as fresh pork UNK and hamburger have a relatively short shelf life under refrigeration , and radiopasteurization might be thought to offer distinctly improved keeping qualities . END
however , a major problem here is one of scale of processing ; ; END
ground meats are usually prepared from scrap meats at the local level , whereas irradiation at economic volumes of production would require central processing and distribution facilities . END
the problems of color change by blanching and liquid accumulation within the package are the same as for solid cuts . END
specialty cooked items containing meat portions , as in `` frozen dinners '' might offer a potential use for radiopasteurization . END
the principal potential advantage would be that the finished product could be transported and stored at lower cost under refrigeration instead of being frozen . END
a refrigerated item could also be heated and served in less time than is required for frozen foods of the same type . END
competitive processes for preserving meats are by canning and freezing . END
costs of canning meat are in the range of 0.8 to 5 cents per pound ; ; END
costs of freezing are in the area of 2 to 3.5 cents per pound . END

the instrument is shown in fig. 1 and consists essentially of a hard , sharp , tungsten UNK knife which is pushed along the substrate to remove the coating . END

if a UNK coating of increasing thickness is removed from a substrate by an instrument like the hesiometer with a knife of constant rake angle , a number of removal mechanisms are often observed which depend upon the thickness of the coating . END
at low UNK a cutting ( or shearing ) phenomenon is often encountered . END
as the coating becomes thicker , the cutting may abruptly change to a cracking type of failure . END
if the coating becomes still thicker , a peeling type failure finally can occur . END
the typical appearance of these various mechanisms is illustrated in figs. 2 , 3 , and 4 , which are single frame UNK of high speed movies taken during the course of the knife removal process . END
it can be seen from fig. 2 that the cutting removal of a coating from its substrate involves pure cohesive failure of the coating . END
the molecular forces holding the coating to the substrate are obviously greater than the cohesive strength of the coating and failure occurs by shear along a plane starting at the tip of the knife and extending to the coating surface . END
the pictures of figs. 3 and 4 show the cracking and peeling types of removal where the coating is detached by failure in a region at , or close to , the UNK between coating and substrate . END
if the force required to remove the coatings is plotted against film thickness , a graph as illustrated UNK in fig. 5 may characteristically result . END
here , h is the coatings removal force measured parallel to the surface of the substrate and t is the film thickness . END
it can be seen that the force is characteristic of the removal process and changes abruptly from cutting to cracking to peeling removal . END
also , it can be readily seen that the cutting and peeling types of failure show a steady state response , while the cracking mechanism is of a dynamic nature . END
it should be recalled that these three mechanisms can occur on the same coating deposited upon the same substrate merely as a function of changes in coatings thickness . END
presumably the interfacial bond strength and gross cohesive properties are identical in each case . END

chipping mechanism of cohesive failure END
although a large number of coatings systems , particularly at low UNK fail UNK by the cutting mechanism , frequently a second type of cohesive failure may also take place . END

this test method is intended for determining the dimensional changes of woven or knitted fabrics , made of fibers other than wool , to be expected when the cloth is subjected to laundering procedures commonly used in the commercial laundry and the home . END

the set of all decisions is called the operating policy or , more simply , the policy . END
an optimal policy is one which in some sense gets the best out of the process as a whole by maximizing the value of the product . END
there are thus three components to an optimal design problem : ( 1 ) END
the UNK of the state of the process stream ; ; END
the UNK of the operating variables and the transformation they effect ; ; END
the UNK of the objective function of which the UNK is desired . END
for a chemical process the first of these might involve the concentrations of the different chemical species , and the temperature or pressure of the stream . END
for the second we might have to choose the volume of reactor or amount of cooling to be supplied ; ; END
the way in which the transformation of state depends on the operating variables for the main types of reactors is discussed in the next chapter . END
the objective function is some measure of the increase in value of the stream by processing ; ; END
it is the subject of chapter 4 . END
the essential characteristic of an optimal policy when the state of the stream is transformed in a sequence of stages with no feedback was first isolated by UNK . END
he recognized that whatever transformation may be effected in the first stage of an r-stage process , the remaining stages must use an optimal af-stage policy with respect to the state resulting from the first stage , if there is to be any chance of UNK the complete process . END
moreover , by systematically varying the operating conditions in the first stage and always using the optimal af-stage policy for the remaining stages , we shall eventually find the optimal policy for all r stages . END
proceeding in this way , from one to two and from two to three stages , we may gradually build up the policy for any number . END
at each step of the calculation the operating variables of only one stage need be varied . END
to see how important this economy is , let us suppose that there are m operating variables at each stage and that the state is specified by n variables ; ; END
then the search for the maximum at any one stage will require a number of operations of order af ( where a is some number not UNK large ) . END
to proceed from one stage to the next a sufficient number of feed states must be investigated to allow for UNK ; ; END
this number will be of the order of af . END
if , however , we are seeking the optimal r-stage policy for a given feed state , only one search for a maximum is required at the final step . END
thus a number of operations of the order of af are required . END
if all the operating variables were varied simultaneously , af operations would be required to do the same job , and as r increases this increases very much more rapidly than the number of operations required by the dynamic program . END
but even more important than this is the fact that the direct search by simultaneously varying all operating conditions has produced only one optimal policy , namely , that for the given feed state and r stages . END
in contrast , the dynamic program produces this policy and a whole family of policies for any smaller number of stages . END
if the problem is enlarged to require a complete coverage of feed states , af operations are needed by the dynamic program and af by the direct search . END
but af is vastly larger than r . END
no optimism is more UNK than that which believes that the high speed of modern digital computers allows for use of the UNK of methods in searching out a result . END
suppose that af , and that the average operation requires only af UNK . END
then the dynamic program would require about a minute whereas the direct search would take more than three UNK ! ! END
the principle of optimality thus brings a vital organization into the search for the optimal policy of a UNK decision process . END
UNK ( 1957 ) has UNK in the following terms : END
`` an optimal policy has the property that whatever the initial state and initial decision are , the remaining decisions must constitute an optimal policy with respect to the state resulting from the first decision '' . END
this is the principle which we will invoke in every case to set up a functional equation . END
it appears in a form that is admirably suited to the powers of the digital computer . END
at the same time , every device that can be employed to reduce the number of variables is of the greatest value , and it is one of the attractive features of dynamic programming that room is left for ingenuity in using the special features of the problem to this end . END

the system as indicated in fig. UNK is fundamental and simple because the UNK effects of both the platform servo and the accelerometer have been neglected . END

in practice , the UNK leveling process takes place with the system mounted in the UNK . END
when the system is arranged for automatic leveling , the platform angles respond to any horizontal components of acceleration acting on the accelerometers . END
there are many such components of acceleration present due to the effect of wind UNK , engine noise , UNK around the vehicle , etc. . END
one of the greatest problems associated with automatic leveling is establishing a true level in the presence of high-level acceleration noise . END
one solution to the problem is to operate with a low loop gain and to include UNK filters . END
this technique causes the system to respond only to low frequency acceleration components such as the platform tilt . END
since a lower loop gain and UNK filtering increases the response time , a practical compromise must be reached . END
one of the most desirable solutions is achieved by the use of a UNK amplifier for af . END
the amplifier is designed so that its gain is large for accelerometer signals above a certain threshold level . END
below this level , the amplifier gain af is proportional and is of small value , in order to provide adequate noise filtering . END
the effect is that the platform returns from an UNK position at a rapid rate until it is nearly level , at which point the platform is controlled by a proportional servo with low enough frequency response so that the noise has little effect on the leveling process . END
when the system is on automatic leveling , the gyro drift is canceled by the output of the leveling system ( amplifier af ) . END
the platform actually UNK off level so that the accelerometer output , when amplified by af , will supply the correct current to the gyro torquer to cancel the gyro drift . END
the amount of platform dip required depends upon the scale factors of the system . END
the automatic leveling system described in this section is readily UNK to a gyro-stabilized platform consisting of three UNK gyros . END
the system requires some switching of flight equipment circuits . END
however , the leveling operation can be maintained and controlled remotely with no mechanical or optical contact with the platform . END
this leveling system will hold the platform UNK , automatically , as long as the system is UNK . END
a useful UNK of this system is that the information necessary to set the gyro drift UNK is available from the currents necessary to hold the system in level . END
the leveling process can be accomplished UNK , and the results are as satisfactory as those obtained with automatic equipment . END
the process consists in turning the platform UNK until the outputs of both accelerometers are zero . END
the turning is accomplished by applying voltage to the gyro torquers described above . END
in brief , the human replaces amplifier af in figs. 7-1 and UNK . END
manual leveling requires an appropriate display of the accelerometer outputs . END
if high accuracy is required in UNK leveling , it is usually necessary to integrate or doubly integrate the accelerometer outputs ( this also UNK the noise problem ) . END
with integration , the effect of a small acceleration ( or small platform tilt angle ) can be seen after a time . END
however , skill is required on the part of an operator to level a platform to any degree of accuracy . END
also , it requires more time as compared to the automatic approach . END
manual leveling is UNK if the platform must be maintained accurately level for any prolonged period of time . END
the operator must continually supply the correct amount of turning current to the gyro torquers so that the effect of gyro drift is canceled . END
this process is especially difficult since gyro drifting is typically random . END
platform heading consists of UNK the sensitive axis of the accelerometers parallel to the desired coordinate system of the UNK . END
in simpler terms , it amounts to pointing the platform in the proper direction . END
for purely UNK UNK , two techniques are available to accomplish the platform heading : use of external or surveying equipment to establish proper heading ; ; END
use of the characteristics of the platform components for an indication of true heading . END
the choice of the heading technique is dependent upon the accuracy requirements , field conditions , and the time available to accomplish the heading . END
external determination of heading -- surveying technique . END
with the gyro-stabilized platform leveled , it can be headed in the proper direction by using surveying techniques . END
the platform accelerometers must be slightly modified for this procedure . END
before the accelerometers are mounted on the platform , the direction of their sensitive axis must be accurately determined . END
a mirror is mounted on each accelerometer so that the plane of the mirror is UNK to the sensitive axis of the unit . END
a precision transit is set up so that it is aligned with respect to true north . END
